Bob, I hope you'll wait a bit and test the new Tekumel rules put out by
Tome. Then you may not need all these rules conversions to other peoples'
systems -- conversions that work but which (for me) do not express some of
Tekumel's specifics very well. I keep seeings "GURPS-Tekumel," "A-D-&-D
Tekumel," "Runequest-Tekumel" -- why not "Monopoly-Tekumel?" -- but not
"Tekumel-Tekumel." This has been largely my fault: too much to do and no
longer the energy, health, or willpower to write yet ANOTHER set of rules
(when I am basically BORED by rules and continue to game Tekumel with
almost no rules at all). But soon we shall have a new set, and I hope
people will give them a try before running off to more familiar territory.

-- 
MikeBarkeley

From rkurgas@inlink.com Tue Apr  4 18:31:34 CDT 1995

From: Rick Kurgas <rkurgas@inlink.com>
Newsgroups: alt.games.frp.tekumel
Subject: Re: Religions And Frp
Date: Fri, 31 Mar 1995 23:08:24 -0600
Organization: inlink
Lines: 45
Message-ID: <Pine.BSD.3.91.950331223717.5155D-100000@vulcan.inlink.com>
References: <796594762.MC4435@mclink.it>
NNTP-Posting-Host: vulcan.inlink.com
Mime-Version: 1.0
Content-Type: TEXT/PLAIN; charset=US-ASCII
In-Reply-To: <796594762.MC4435@mclink.it> 

	Religion may not be inherent to some FRP games, but it is 
essential to Tekumel, because the "gods" really exist, at least within 
the game.  True, they are not really gods, but they are so powerful that 
the sentient beings on that world (at least, in the known world) regard 
these almost-omnipotents AS gods.
	Various source books of Professor Barker's try to point this out 
about Tekumel's culture, that even though they are operating under a 
delusion, it's probably a healthy one.  For example, if enough people on 
that world told the "gods" to kiss off, these same "gods" would most 
likely destroy that section of the planet.  This would certainly instill 
respect in the survivors, and is most probably what happened to Engsvan 
hla Ganga.
	After a period of time, most folks would probably only give 
lip-service to these "gods", but the people who really matter -- the 
priests and politicians -- would still have strong faith in them, due to 
their actual contact with their "gods".  They probably have also given 
and received favors from each other, enforcing their relationship.  And 
since they're in charge, the masses who don't care at least have to 
pretend they do.
	In a world like this, you could roleplay a character who doesn't 
really believe in "gods", but just goes through the motions of 
obeisance.  But don't expect the character to stay cynically aloof for 
too long, because sooner or later, (s)he would directly experience the 
presence of a "god" or "goddess".  The character could still pretend that 
(s)he didn't have a numinous experience, but we all know the character 
would be lying to themselves.
	Staying an atheist in a Tekumel campaign means the GM is 
deliberately witholding any meetings between the characters and the 
"gods".  This is definitely NOT in the spirit of the game.  An adventurer 
should have had at least one encounter with a "god" or "goddess" while 
wandering the expanses of both known and unknown Tekumel.  More likely, 
three or four.
	These can create some of the most interesting roleplaying in the 
game, in exact opposition to the other FRPG, which seems to devalue their 
gods to the point where they're wimps for any high-level party to take 
out.  Not so in Tekumel.  These "gods" don't even THINK like the 
characters do!  The most powerful person on Tekumel is a flea compared to 
their power and knowledge.
	A Tekumeli might be able to trick a "god" on occasion, due to their 
different ways of thinking, but don't even try to best them at their own 
games.  Such foolishness tends to make for short life-spans.  Or 
extremely long, torturous ones.  These "gods" do like their sport.


				Rick K.

In article <3lmoih$puj@newsbf02.news.aol.com>, mabelrode@aol.com
(Mabelrode) wrote:

>      I have a question regarding skills in the White Book for Character
> Generation book Sec 9.3 hobby skills.  Weapon Skills are listed there can
> a character take these at the reduced cost and apply toward the warrior
> skills
> that are listed on the character sheet.  This also true for the soldering
> skills and other professional and academic skills that could be bought at
> lower costs in the hobby skills section.
> 
> Sincerely
> 
> David Peters (Mabelrode)

I had intended this to be so -- but at a reduced rate. On p. 41 I wrote,
"... you may convert relevant hobby skills into spells or warrior weapons
at a rate of 3 hobby skills = 1 spell or 1 weapon level." This was clear
enough to me, and I meant it to apply to all hobby skills (soldier, etc.).
Neil Cauley (my co-author) says that there is an ambiguity here, and we
will probably fix it in a rules error-list. At the moment I can't see what
it is. Buy 3 hobby levels of some warrior weapon, and count it as 1 level
of actual fighting skill. Etc.

--

From barke002@maroon.tc.umn.edu Tue Apr  4 18:31:37 CDT 1995

Organization: University of Minnesota, Twin Cities

Date: Sun, 2 Apr 1995 20:25:36 GMT
Lines: 26

In article <3lmoo3$q0b@newsbf02.news.aol.com>, mabelrode@aol.com
(Mabelrode) wrote:

>      I have another question regarding aquiring skills from the book, does
> a priestly character gain more points for skills while going up the ranks
> in the
> priesthood in section C.1 and C.2.
> 
> 
> Sincerely
> 
> David Peters (Mabelrode)

Secs. C.1 and C.2 in Book Two involve promotions within the temple
hierarchy, not the acquisition of spells. You go up in rank but not in
knowledge (sounds like some professors I used to know ...!). You need to
rise in rank because only in this way will you meet people of power, get
access to libraries and ancient artefacts, and make your way in the
political world that passes for the priesthoods on Tekumel. Actual spells
for "adventuring" are available from Sec. C.6, using the same educational
process you used in Book One, Sec. 8.5.

All of this needs cleaning up somewhat -- and will be eventually. Sorry!

-- 
M. A. R. Barker

Rick Kurgas (rkurgas@inlink.com) wrote:

: 	In a world like this, you could roleplay a character who doesn't

: deliberately witholding any meetings between the characters and the 
: "gods".  This is definitely NOT in the spirit of the game.  An adventurer 
: should have had at least one encounter with a "god" or "goddess" while 
: wandering the expanses of both known and unknown Tekumel.  More likely, 
: three or four.

While the point about atheism, etc...is well taken, I still would have to 
say that it is possible for a person in Tekumel to have direct contact 
with "deity" or its manifestations, while still remaining relatively 
secular in attitude.

The best example of this is given in Professor Barker's book flamesong. 
Trinesh (If that was the main character's name, its been a while) begins 
the work as a rather secular moderate of Lord Vimuhla. He encounters 
Flamesong, a divine artifact, in the story. Yet, at the end, he is able 
to appreciate the Mihalli, Aluja's statement that the Gods are merely 
very powerful being who can effectively muck up the world. The best thing 
for humans to do is avoid involving them in their affairs, and kick them 
out once they have become as powerful as the Gods are. Trinesh and his 
second in command appreciate this view, though they don't start spreading 
it around. 

They do not become instant devout believers due to their experience.

As far as the theological system of Tekumel goes, and has been stated 
earlier, these Gods did not create anything (life, the world, etc). This 
leaves the basis for worship to be a combination of fear and greed. Fear 
in that they are the biggest, baddest mamma jamma's around, so if they 
want worship, that's what they get. Greed because they offer power, via 
spell and knowledge to their worshipers. This is not a basis that would 
inspire the higher ups, or any intellectual elite to follow them out of 
devotion, more out of will to power.

Also remember that humanity (as well as Shenity, Pe Choity, etc...) were 
once far more powerful than they are now. The creations of the ancients 
are a reminder of this fact, and would tend to reinforce the idea that 
man (etc...) could potentially become greater than it is now, or regain 
what it has lost.

Finally, there is the existance of beings who do not appear to be in the 
thrall of the deities as much as the "normal" species of Tekumel. The 
Mihalli are one of the best examples. This would also tend to drive home 
the idea that it is possible to not worship, yet still be free to live as 
one would. Maybe no for humanity now, but perhaps someday.

All of these factors would indicate to me that true devotion, or 
fanaticism (such as that of Trinesh's Vriddi Chaplain) would be 
relatively less common than moderate to secular views, though actual 
public athiesm would be near non-existant.

Washington D.C. 20009-1451          : yes, you know he is crooked"- GM

Well, I have already pre-ordered by Gardasiyal set, and I will certainly 
give it a once over and perhaps a run or two.  But lets face it, the real
attraction of Tekumel is the setting, not the game mechanics.  As you have
(from what I hear) already discovered, mechanics-heavy systems are just not
as effective as a lightweight, streamlined, little-or-no-rules kinda setup.

Right now, I am setting up my game using the mechanics from Over The Edge
(side note: right now I am calling this jezu mikqapa -- does this strike
you as a reasonable translation?) because of its heavy emphasis on 
character and setting.  I am mainly looking for Gardasiyal's magic system
info, since I have EPT but not S&G vol2.

OTOH, I hope Gardasiyal sells like all getout.  Need to get the pipeline
moving, and a new system always helps.

In article <3lmp5t$q37@newsbf02.news.aol.com>, mabelrode@aol.com
(Mabelrode) wrote:

>      Will there be rules for generating non-human characters in the new
> rules
> for Tekumel.  I hope it willl not be for a future rulesbook>
> 
> 
> Sincerely
> 
> David Peters (Mabelrode)

Yes, the first book of the new rules set has the differences in stats for
nonhuman characters. The third book also has some stuff for nonhuman
NPCSs. You'll find it helpful, I hope! You still can't roll up a
player-character Serudla or Sro (or Ssu, Hluss, or other inimical
species), but you can do most of the friendly intelligent species. In our
gaming groups we rarely try to play nonhumans -- the mind-sets are too
different, and we don't want to be just "Americans in alien costumes." But
I have seen very good Pe Choi characters, a few passable Shen, and a
couple of Tinaliya that were so exasperating that they deserved worse than
they got!

-- 
M. A. R. Barker

From sfoster@cix.compulink.co.uk Tue Apr  4 18:31:41 CDT 1995
Article: 253 of alt.games.frp.tekumel
Newsgroups: alt.games.frp.tekumel
Path: illuminati.io.com!uunet!pipex!uknet!cix.compulink.co.uk!usenet
From: sfoster@cix.compulink.co.uk ("Stephen Foster")
Subject: Re: Religions And Frp
Message-ID: <D6H6KF.1HK@cix.compulink.co.uk>
Organization: Unknown Organization          
References: <barke002-3103951836410001@dialup-2-87.gw.umn.edu>
Date: Mon, 3 Apr 1995 20:07:26 GMT
X-News-Software: Ameol
Lines: 20

In our world, religions are usually used to wield power, i.e. the priest 
classes threaten retribution and the followers kow-tow. If a natural 
disaster happens then the priests use this as "proof" of the power of 
their god(s). After 5,000 years or so, the followers get wise to these 
tricks. They call the priests' bluff, overthrow them and set up a 
rational, secular society. (Finally happened in about 1860 in our world 
when Origin of the Species was published)

On Tekumel, things must be slightly different. Priests still wield power, 
but the threat of divine retribution is real! Every 5,000 years or so the 
followers *think* that they've gotten wise, call the priests' bluff and 
get obliterated. They never get to set up a rational, secular society, 
and one of the major causes of technological, moral and social change is 
eliminated.

It's no wonder that cultures on Tekumel stagnate for millenia (... in 
technological, moral and social terms; they may be very dynamic societies 
in artistic, cultural and other matters).

Steve F


From woodelf@yar.cs.wisc.edu Tue Apr  4 18:31:42 CDT 1995

Subject: Re: fonts/typefaces
Date: 3 Apr 1995 23:44:25 GMT
Organization: UW-Madtown
Lines: 42
Sender: -Not-Authenticated-[7358]
Message-ID: <3lq18p$eqm@news.doit.wisc.edu>
References: <3kjida$5kd@news.bt.net> 
 <barke002-2203951833490001@dialup-4-68.gw.umn.edu> 
 <3l1imi$fq2@mace.cc.purdue.edu> 
 <barke002-2703952111080001@dialup-3-201.gw.umn.edu> 
 <3lfrjo$e28@news.doit.wisc.edu>  
 <barke002-3103951806510001@dialup-2-87.gw.umn.ed>
NNTP-Posting-Host: f180-118.net.wisc.edu
X-Posted-From: InterNews 1.0.1@f180-118.net.wisc.edu
Xdisclaimer: No attempt was made to authenticate the sender's name.

In article <barke002-3103951806510001@dialup-2-87.gw.umn.edu>
barke002@maroon.tc.umn.edu writes:

> A gentleman in Washington, D. C., has developed a special piece of
> keyboard software for Maya glyphs. One types TWO keys for each glyph:
> nothing appears when the first key is struck, but the whole glyph appears
> when the second key hits. His solution is monument-specific: each stela or
> inscription has its own "font," and one types A-1 for the top left glyph,
> then A-2 for the glyph underneath that, B-1 for the top glyph in the
> second column, etc. etc. I would need something like this to do some of
> the absolutely daunting fonts, like the Secret Tongues of Ksarul and
> Sarku. They have large fonts of rather complex glyphs, and I am not about
> to try to squeeze all of them onto a keyboard! I should get a life!!!

i'm not sure how it's done, or if the various commercial
font-developement programs support it, but there are such things as
2-byte fonts for the Mac, and Windows 95 will support Unicode, which
does the same thing.  in both cases, you get 256x256 characters by
requiring two keystrokes, which combine to pick the character.  it's
much like you mentioned, except that it's a part of the operating
system, and so should work in any application.  therefore, all you'd
need is the font and your favorite word processor.  again, i'm not sure
how to create a font that utilizes this.  also, for the mac you'd need
the Japanese language kit, i think, and either something similar for
the PC, or wait for windows 95.

however, i just remembered that there is one other solution, at least
on a mac.  i'm not sure, but i suspect that it can also be done somehow
on a PC.  in a mac font, you can designate a character as appearing
over the next character that is typed.  thus, there is a circumflex
character, for example, and you type it, followed by a vowel, say "a",
and you get "a circumflex".  this is the standard way it deals with
european diacreticals.  This doesn't address the right-to-left problem,
but it might help with squeezing enough characters into a 256-character
font.

hope some of the above helps.

woodelf
nbarmore@students.wisc.edu
woodelf@yar.cs.wisc.edu
http://yar.cs.wisc.edu/~woodelf

From rdushay@delphi.com Tue Apr  4 18:31:43 CDT 1995
Article: 256 of alt.games.frp.tekumel
Path: illuminati.io.com!uunet!news.delphi.com!usenet
From: Robert Dushay <rdushay@delphi.com>

Subject: Re: Religions And Frp
Date: Mon, 3 Apr 95 20:40:16 -0500
Organization: Delphi (info@delphi.com email, 800-695-4005 voice)
Lines: 18
Message-ID: <B02dIaI.rdushay@delphi.com>
References: <796594762.MC4435@mclink.it> <3lh630$1pu@cedar.mr.net>
NNTP-Posting-Host: bos1d.delphi.com
X-To: Bob Alberti <alberti@riverside.mr.net>

Debates over imaginary religions can get so fascinating.  Piermaria may
have made a mistake in assuming that a religion has to make sense--in fact,
if religion simply explained the laws of the universe and how it was created,
it would be more like science--a rule book of creation, if you will.
I think religion is more satisfying to practitioners if there is a sense
of awe and mystery to it, a feeling that some things must be left to the
individual to discover.  More importantly, I don't think humans should be
able to comprehend all of the facets of creation, even if there is a single,
logical, coherent system that religion explains.  If humans could comprehend
this, what would separate them from their gods?  Gods would not be nearly
so worship-worthy if they were no smarter or omniscient than humans, merely
more powerful.  Of course, Tekumel is unique among RPG universes, to my
knowledge, where the gods are real, but they are not true deities in the
way that we currently understand the word.
 
  Just my 1.5 cents.                          --Bob Dushay
                                    Dritlan, Legion of Obscure Books
                                    1st Imperial Heavy Scholars

I ran a D&D game, using their relatively wimpy 'gods' that could be taken 
out by a party of powerful PCs, but presented my players with an 'atheistic 
religion' that denied the divinity of these beings, seeing them as no 
more than very very powerful bullies.  The central teaching of this 
religion was that all beings ought to respect the rights of lesser ones, 
and not exploit them.  Of course, this message was perverted by the royal 
government into a mandate to go out and 'liberate' any neighbors that 
happened to be theists...

In article <3lqvk1$298@sun.sirius.com>, ysidro@sirius.com (ysidro) wrote:

> Greetings. I have on occassion seen a list of the various Tekumel 
> products distributed throughout the history of the game. I haven't seen 
> an annotated list, one that describes the content of the various 
> products. Has one been made? Of special value to me would be an annotated 
> list that also compared the products to each other.
> 
> Also, when is the TOME product being released, and where does it fit in 
> comparison to the lineage of earlier products? (Is it a complete set of 
> rules and background, or rules only, etc etc.)
> 
> Thank you, and I would love replies via email in addition to posted 
> replies; I do not visit newsgroups very often. 
> 
> Thanks!
> 
> ysidro@sirius.com

You can ask TOME to provide information. Essentially, there is the
Bestiary (critters and societally challenged alien minority groups), 3
vols. of solitaire adventures (which provide lots of background), possibly
a few (very few!) copies of the first 2 vols. of the old Tekumel
Sourcebook, and coming soon -- late April -- the new rules. These are
completely new rules, though based roughly on Sword and Glory. They
include the usual: movement, combat, rolling up npcs, rolling up alien
characters, combat, magic, lots of new magic items, books, etc., and some
stuff for referees. New map, new art, etc. The map should be out this week
and is the last item to be included in the fancy box. For background, you
and others may want to bug TOME to reprint the earlier Sourcebook in its
entirety.

-- 
M. A. R. Barker

Actually, I have already ordered TWO copies of Gardasiyal, and I have
hungrily devoured every Tekumel-Tekumel rules set published!  My AD&D rules
were originally designed to try to bring EPT more up to date, and I have
made the effort to post them primarily for those folks who have players
who would like to try Tekumel, but don't want to invest in the new rules.

is NOT a stand-alone product, and that it is highly recommended that
referees have access to the Tekumel rules sets.  I am also going to run
a demo adventure using the new rules at a local games store as soon as
possible.  So don't worry--I will be giving the new rules a very thorough
check-through, and I intend to encourage as many people as I can to give
them a try as well.

From anton@infoserv.com Mon Apr 10 18:50:49 CDT 1995
Article: 276 of alt.games.frp.tekumel
Path: news.io.com!uunet!news1.digital.com!nntp-hub2.barrnet.net!nntp-sc.barrnet.net!infoserv!anton!anton
Message-ID: <766215d8381261t184@anton.infoserv.com>
Date: Friday, 7 April 1995 21:18:53 PDT
X-Mailer: TMail version 1.13
From: "Anton Dovydaitis" <anton@infoserv.com>
Organization: American Youth GO Association
Subject: Making Prof. Barker Money
Newsgroups: alt.games.frp.tekumel
Distribution: world
Lines: 116

I think nearly all users of this newsgroup would agree that effectively
disseminating Tekumel at the same time that Professor Barker gets handsful
of money would be a good thing.  I think they'd also agree that this is
not quite happening.  I do not see Tekumel in many stores in the Bay Area.
Barker's world is a superior product: the vision usually reserved for
a literary figure, combined with the intellectual rigor of a social
scientist.  So why hasn't it been more successful in the market?

First of, before people think I'm sounding too critical, let me say
that I appreciate everyone's efforts to bring Tekumel to the market.
I have spoken with my dollars, I own nearly everything produced by or
on behalf of Barker, and I am delighted with what I have.  I feel
fortunate to have had access to his volumnia.

But it seems to me that an essentially iconoclastic rules systems is
counter-productive media to market Tekumel, whose best appeal is 
its total vision.  Roleplaying has evolved to the point where the
best rules DON'T get in the way.  While Barker often exhorts the
utility of on-the-fly refereeing, his published rules sets to date
are too cumbersome to be appealing to the current generation of
roleplayers, and this serves to impede market adaption.

Instead, it is the WORLD of Tekumel that must be sold, the images
of an alien but enduring civilization, more detailed and realistic
than Tolkein was in toto.  Tekumel's selling strength is its richness
of detail, the thoroughness of Barker's work.  Whereas as Tolkein
was a linguist, Barker is a more rounded social scientist.  Middle
Earth is a sterile place, there is no religion, but Tekumel fairly
stinks of its people.  Social interaction is the foundation of role
playing: people in a room discussing what other people would or are
doing in another situation.  Barker brings a much more convincing
description of what people do under various conditions than nearly
any other role playing world I have seen.  Tekumel might not be
possible physically, but it seems plausible socially.  In a game
that centers around character interaction, this is an important
plus (it's kind of hard to relate, in any deep way, with a Palladin
on a Quest for a +5 Holy Avenger).

I bought and will continue to buy Tekumel because I no longer have
the time to invent so widely, but I want to roleplay in a vivid,
detailed world that is not historical.  Barker's is the most complete
and well developed world on the market.

I would submit that the best media for Tekumel would be as reference
materials, as free from formal rules as possible.  This would lead
with Tekumel's main strength, and not being tied to any particular
rules, would enjoy a broader market.  This would allow more referees
to adopt it, which leads to more players, which leads to a larger
market.  I am afraid that by coming out with the solo adventures
first, TOME may have actually sabotaged the market for the rules set.
Sell someone a game they can't play, and they won't come back.
After nearly half a dozen such catastrophes, only us hardcore
fanatics remain.

If I were in charge of marketing Tekumel, here is what I'd do.
First, I'd suck up everything Barker ever authorized printing,
and suck it up into an optical scanner.  I'd cross-reference
the hell out of it, and hire an Anthropology or Sociology 
grad student (a good one, one that teach) to summarize the
general structure of it.  At the same time, I'd start showing
some of the stuff to artists, and sell them on.  Not necessarily
hire them, mind you, but impress upon them the vision of this alien
world.  There are a lot of poor but talented artists out there,
especially young ones, and I'd eventually hit the jackpot.

Paper is still superior to CD ROM, both in terms of utility and
market penetration, so I'd publish an Encyclopedia Tekumel
(something they would never do themselves).  I could get sales
at $29.95, especially if it was richly illustrated and complete
in the essential details.  People pay more than that regular
for some god-awful crap, and would be delighted to have something
of real value for that.  I would format it much more like an
encyclopedia, with maps, diagrams, tables, even the summary of
linguistics.  Think of how much money TSR makes selling endless
false maps of those ridiculous charicatures of worlds (Forgotten
Realms and Greyhawk).

In order to recover more money earlier, I would sell limited edition
numbered leathbound drafts at a higher price to people like myself.
They would have more mistakes than the final, but fanatics would love
to have the material.  The fanatics (and I count myself among them)
would use the material to prepare the larger market by incorporating
it into games and showing it off to their friends.  If Tekumel ever
did prove to be substantially more popular, these early editions would
also become collector's items, adding to the mystique.

Given the current state of affairs, this is what I would do
immediately.  I haven't seen the new map yet, but I can tell you
I still remember pulling the first map out of original EPT boxed
set: that thing was gorgeous!  TOME may have some trouble convincing
stores to stock their $45.00 RPG sets, but the map and a brief color
booklet would sell like hotcakes at under $20, and would sell, but
not nearly as well, as a thicker book and map for $25.  As a $14.95
color poster map of Tekumel framed by text and images, well, I could
sell those hand over fist.

Then later I could sell those Encyclopedias, maybe more than one
volume, at $29.95, easy.

I understand that TOME can't really do this directly now: they need
as much of that $45 as they can get to recover their costs.  But the
market is very small, and not as many of them are going to sell as
TOME would like.

Pictures, images, words, a vision: that's what sells about Tekumel,
not rules.

Again, I am not saying that I believe the product to be inferior,
or that I did not receive fair value for my money, I'm just saying
that the market for the current products is too narrow, and the
market for other products could be much broader.


Anton Dovydaitis			anton@infoserv.com
  For Ishi Press inquiries, mail to `ishius@ishius.com'.


From resmith@donald.cc.utexas.edu Mon Apr 10 18:50:50 CDT 1995

From: resmith@donald.cc.utexas.edu (Robert Smith)
Newsgroups: alt.games.frp.tekumel
Subject: Ghatoni Legions, question?
Date: 9 Apr 1995 11:00:42 -0500
Organization: The University of Texas at Austin; Austin, Texas
Lines: 19
Message-ID: <3m90ba$2ci@donald.cc.utexas.edu>
NNTP-Posting-Host: donald.cc.utexas.edu

As some of you know, John Medway and I are developing a new set of miniatures
rules for Tekumel. For most troop types, the ratings are fairly obvious. But
the Ghatoni have become problematic. 

Ghatoni troops are described as ferocious but undisciplined fighters. At first
this would seem to call for them to be rated as irregular (which in our rules
means they are prone to charging off toward the enemy and more difficult to 
manuever). But illustrations show them in uniform dress, a sign of more 
regular troops. So what is the answer, regular (well trained, controlled,
easy to manuever) or irregular? If the latter, should they really be
uniformly equipped, or was this artistic license? 

Continuing on this line of inquiry, the YK army book lists the Ghatoni as 
swordsmen, but the illustrations show warriors equipped with light spears.
Again, which is correct?

Any advice will be greatly appreciated.

Rob

Subject: Re: Ghatoni Legions, question?

Organization: University of Minnesota, Twin Cities

References: <3m90ba$2ci@donald.cc.utexas.edu>
Date: Mon, 10 Apr 1995 17:26:40 GMT
Lines: 23

 Ghatoni troops are described as ferocious but undisciplined fighters. At first
> this would seem to call for them to be rated as irregular (which in our rules
> means they are prone to charging off toward the enemy and more difficult to 
> manuever). But illustrations show them in uniform dress, a sign of more 
> regular troops. So what is the answer, regular (well trained, controlled,
> easy to manuever) or irregular? If the latter, should they really be
> uniformly equipped, or was this artistic license? 
> 
> Continuing on this line of inquiry, the YK army book lists the Ghatoni as 
> swordsmen, but the illustrations show warriors equipped with light spears.
> Again, which is correct?

Both are true: they are "irregular" in tye sense that they do not use the
complex, highly disciplined formations employed bby the Five Empires, but
they also have uniforms and weapons supplied by the cities and towns that
supply them. So their uniforms are "regular." They just lack training but
make up for it in bravery and ferocity. Don't limit troops to either
illustrations or to the brief descriptions: the Ghatoni fight well with
swords, but they also have light spears. This is one of the signs of their
"irregularity."

-- 
M. A. R. Barker

From alberti@riverside.mr.net Mon Apr 10 18:50:59 CDT 1995
Article: 284 of alt.games.frp.tekumel
Path: news.io.com!news.tamu.edu!bloom-beacon.mit.edu!spool.mu.edu!howland.reston.ans.net!math.ohio-state.edu!scipio.cyberstore.ca!skypoint.com!news3.mr.net!mr.net!riverside.mr.net!alberti
From: alberti@riverside.mr.net (Bob Alberti)
Newsgroups: alt.games.frp.tekumel
Subject: Re: Making Prof. Barker Money
Date: 10 Apr 1995 20:09:59 GMT
Organization: Minnesota Regional Network
Lines: 117
Distribution: world
Message-ID: <3mc3an$67m@cedar.mr.net>
References: <766215d8381261t184@anton.infoserv.com>
NNTP-Posting-Host: riverside.mr.net
X-Newsreader: TIN [version 1.2 PL2]

Anton Dovydaitis (anton@infoserv.com) wrote:
: I do not see Tekumel in many stores in the Bay Area.

Interestingly enough, I just found Tome's products at our local Dreamhaven
F/SF bookstore.  I was quite happily surprised!

: Barker's world is a superior product: the vision usually reserved for
: a literary figure, combined with the intellectual rigor of a social
: scientist.  So why hasn't it been more successful in the market?

Uh...  er..  doesn't your first sentence there answer your second?
P. T. Barnum could explain it to you...

: Instead, it is the WORLD of Tekumel that must be sold, the images
: of an alien but enduring civilization, more detailed and realistic
: than Tolkein was in toto.  Tekumel's selling strength is its richness
: of detail, the thoroughness of Barker's work.  Whereas as Tolkein
: was a linguist, Barker is a more rounded social scientist.  Middle
: Earth is a sterile place, there is no religion, but Tekumel fairly
: stinks of its people.

This is the first time I've seen a product lauded with the phrase,
"It stinks!"

: I am afraid that by coming out with the solo adventures
: first, TOME may have actually sabotaged the market for the rules set.
: Sell someone a game they can't play, and they won't come back.
: After nearly half a dozen such catastrophes, only us hardcore
: fanatics remain.

Tekumel certainly suffers from a lack of adequate marketing.
I don't know enough about the FRPG business to say for sure,
but part of the problem from my point of view is that Tekumel
IS different.  It can't be tossed into the "dragons and paladins"
scene AT ALL.

Additionally, Tekumel is a work of art and not a game.  That's 
its real problem in my mind.  Tekumel is a social artwork, it's
a ship-in-a-bottle dreamed up by a man with a unique set of skills.
To mass-market Tekumel, you have to give it to big, established company.
Big, established companies have a product pipeline to support, and turning
out "art" is not part of the scene.

To do Tekumel right, a large company would need to allow itself to be
educated as to exactly what the game is.  Either that, or they need to
invest in Professor Barker, without attempting to control the product.
Most aren't willing to do that.  Tome, a smaller company, was.

: If I were in charge of marketing Tekumel, here is what I'd do.
: First, I'd suck up everything Barker ever authorized printing,
: and suck it up into an optical scanner.  I'd cross-reference
: the hell out of it, and hire an Anthropology or Sociology 
: grad student (a good one, one that teach) to summarize the
: general structure of it.  At the same time, I'd start showing
: some of the stuff to artists, and sell them on.  Not necessarily
: hire them, mind you, but impress upon them the vision of this alien
: world.  There are a lot of poor but talented artists out there,
: especially young ones, and I'd eventually hit the jackpot.

:  "in charge", "scanner", "hire", "showing", "sell"...

The problem here is that's what we'd all like to do who are involved
in any way in the production of Barker's stuff.  The problem is
1) you're never in charge, it's always compromise, 2) scanning and
correlating stuff TAKES TIME, and if no one is drawing a paycheck
to do it, then you have to do it in your spare time, 3) hiring people
takes a lot more resources than anyone has 4) self-marketing (showing)
the product takes lots of time, money and energy, and 5) you can't
sell anyone on anything without money.


I for one appreciate your frustration with the glacial pace at which 
Tekumel products come out.  But life is that you get bogged down in
minutiae -- you scan in the page, and something hiccoughs, and now
one more day is spent rebuilding your computer or debugging your
scanner, and the whole thing gets pushed back a day.

What really needs to happen is that a big game company, a real
Daddy Warbucks comes along and says "Ah, Professor Barker, here's
a full art, marketing, and sales staff, you just sit there and 
write, and if your computer has a problem we'll send in one of our
hardware support personnel."  

Seeing as that's not likely, here's the flip side:  one of the benefits
for the slow way in which the game is produced is, to sound naieve, 
artistic integrity.  Barker's stuff comes out slow because it's
his, not someone else's.  It hasn't been molded to fit someone else's
view of the world.

[more big plans omitted]

Your big plans are great, but as pointed out before there are problems.
First off...  INVESTMENT CAPITAL.  What you're talking about is 
EXPENSIVE.  Go line up someone to pitch in the cash, and be prepared
to give most of it back to them.

Second, if you think you can do this, contact Professor Barker and
pitch it to him.  I'm sure he'd love to see these things take place.
These big ideas take lots of work that is not immediately rewarded.

: Pictures, images, words, a vision: that's what sells about Tekumel,
: not rules.

I think you're a hundred percent right about this.

: Again, I am not saying that I believe the product to be inferior,
: or that I did not receive fair value for my money, I'm just saying
: that the market for the current products is too narrow, and the
: market for other products could be much broader.

This may be true.  I think the underlying reality is that unless
someone were being paid to do these things on a full-time basis,
the current slow pace of production is bound to be maintained.

--
Bob Alberti            "I'm just a mirror of a mirror of myself" -Emily Saliers
alberti@mr.net                 Indigo Girls _Swamp Ophelia_ "Least Complicated"

> Uh...  er..  doesn't your first sentence there answer your second?

No, the statement BEGS the question.  WHY isn't it in the stores?

> P. T. Barnum could explain it to you...

Why don't you explain what you mean here.

If a sucker is born every minute, then why ain't the rubes buying it?

> : Earth is a sterile place, there is no religion, but Tekumel fairly
> : stinks of its people.
> 
> This is the first time I've seen a product lauded with the phrase,
> "It stinks!"

If you've ever been in a truly crowded place, you'll notice that people
stink.  Compared to other game worlds, Tekumel TEAMS with people.

> 
> Tekumel certainly suffers from a lack of adequate marketing.

It's not simply a lack of marketing, as I argue, but trying to
sell the wrong product.

> but part of the problem from my point of view is that Tekumel
> IS different.  It can't be tossed into the "dragons and paladins"
> scene AT ALL.

Instead, that is in fact part of its VALUE.  It may never be mass
market, but there is a significant market of older gamers that
appreciate sophisticated work.  Tekumel can appeal to a larger market.
> 
> Additionally, Tekumel is a work of art and not a game.  That's 
> its real problem in my mind.  Tekumel is a social artwork, it's
> a ship-in-a-bottle dreamed up by a man with a unique set of skills.

Yes, Tekumel is a piece of art, of social fiction, and it should be
marketed as such.

> To mass-market Tekumel, you have to give it to big, established company.
> Big, established companies have a product pipeline to support, and turning
> out "art" is not part of the scene.

No big established company, with in my mind the possible exception of
Chaosium, would ever take on Tekumel.  Most big companies have their
own rule sets, and the modules tied to them.  The other obvious exception
is Steve Jackson Games, the machine that eats liscences.  But as much as
I respect Steve Jackson, it would not be appropriate for him to do it.
It would simply become another unsupported module for GURPS.

No, I think a small company could either do it themselves, or raise the
capital to take on the development costs, then farm out the finished
product to a larger company.  I would argue that Chaosium would be
decent possibility for that, as at two of the games they publish have
a distinct social science angle: RuneQuest, and especially Pendragon.
Also, the people who run Chaosium as well as the main contributors 
are very intelligent and educated people, who would respect Barker. 

> To do Tekumel right, a large company would need to allow itself to be
> educated as to exactly what the game is.

This is actually unnecessary.  I believe that the fans could capitalize
the product development, and then sell the finished or near finished
product to, for example, Chaosium.  If I were a game company, and some
one offered me a well crafted, nearly finished product, I'd factor in
the small development cost in my decision whether to market it.

> Either that, or they need to invest in Professor Barker, without
> attempting to control the product.

This is impossible, and not worth trying.  The people who already
understand Barker's value are the ones who should capitalize development.

> Most aren't willing to do that.  Tome, a smaller company, was.

In my opinion, TOME didn't exert enough editorial or marketing
control over Prof. Barker.
> 
> : If I were in charge of marketing Tekumel...
> 
> :  "in charge", "scanner", "hire", "showing", "sell"...
> 
> The problem here is that's what we'd all like to do who are involved
> in any way in the production of Barker's stuff.  The problem is
> 1) you're never in charge, it's always compromise

I have no idea how to get around this, but I think if I waved enough
money around, things would start to happen.

> 2) scanning and
> correlating stuff TAKES TIME, and if no one is drawing a paycheck
> to do it, then you have to do it in your spare time 3) hiring people

I think you could get volunteers.  I know that if it were legally
possible to do so, I would personally, and in less than 90 days,
scan in everything I owned (which, while not EVERYTHING, is a lot).

> takes a lot more resources than anyone has

So far...

>4) self-marketing (showing)
> the product takes lots of time, money and energy, and 5) you can't
> sell anyone on anything without money.

Ahhh...money...that is the question.

And the answer is: the money IS out there!  Yes.  I KNOW it is.
Here's how to raise it.

I know there are people willing to pay a great deal of money of Barker's
stuff.  I know, because I buy EPT stuff at Flea Markets and odd game
stores, and re-sell it on the InterNet for a handsome profit.  So far,
it's about enough to pay for all the stuff I kept for my personal
collection.  Old Tekumel stuff is about as hot as old RuneQuest stuff.
So there are some deep pockets out there chasing Tekumel.
For example, I COULD get my hands on a typewritten review copy of Swords and
Glory signed by Barker.  Guess how much I could get for it?  More than
a Black Lotus, I can tell you that.

In a real business, I would try to sell STOCK in such a venture, but I
can't.  It's not a large enough business.  However, I know there are
people who overvalue Barker.  If I could sell something to them to raise
money, there are those who would buy it.  I'd put $100 without thinking,
and depending on how you marketed it, you could string a lot of people
along say $25-$100 at a time.

What I'd sell are preliminary or review copies of the various products,
maybe in pieces (for example, a book in three parts), and make it limited
edition, signed by Barker.  Good production values are easy to obtain
with laser printers and copy places.

> I for one appreciate your frustration with the glacial pace at which 
> Tekumel products come out.  But life is that you get bogged down in
> minutiae -- you scan in the page, and something hiccoughs, and now
> one more day is spent rebuilding your computer or debugging your
> scanner, and the whole thing gets pushed back a day.

There needs to be more people working on it.  I'm certain that there
are poor fanatics who could not afford to invest money in Barker,
can could invest time, and who have access to computers.  If the
text was available electronically, then a larger number of people
could collaborate on it, sorting, indexing, etc. 
> 
> What really needs to happen is that a big game company...

Not going to happen...

> [more big plans omitted]
> 
> Your big plans are great, but as pointed out before there are problems.
> First off...  INVESTMENT CAPITAL.  What you're talking about is 
> EXPENSIVE.  Go line up someone to pitch in the cash, and be prepared
> to give most of it back to them.

I can do it.

Outline your capital needs: what equipment you need, what marginal
income would enable the main participants to devote significant
time to the project.  Consider what work could be done by other volunteers,
especially menial work based on already published sources.

Be realistic and fair.  The money would be raised from fans, not a big
game company.  But if it made the difference, and SOMETHING could be
produced as a return on the investment, then it would fly.

> Second, if you think you can do this, contact Professor Barker and
> pitch it to him.

I imagine he reads this newsgroup.  As it is, my newsreader won't let
me read or compose outside it, so I can't easily re-email this to him.

 
> This may be true.  I think the underlying reality is that unless
> someone were being paid to do these things on a full-time basis,
> the current slow pace of production is bound to be maintained.

It may well require one person full time, and I doubt that it could
be Barker (unless he's retired or owns significant real estate).
But consider better use of volunteers.  I'm sure you'd find many
eager people willing to help.  Also, graduate students are made to
be abused: I suggest Barker consider doing so.  It would be a legitimate
exercise to have a student examine and summarize Barker's work as a
piece of social fiction from the perspective of social science.
Sounds like a thesis to me.

Let me think about the numbers.  Basically, I think a lot of people
would pay at least $25 a chunk for the promise of limited edition
EPT stuff in the future, but maybe $100 is a better point.  And those
who can't afford to invest can afford to work.

I've helped make an awful lot of things happen in my life so far, and I'd
be happy to add one more thing to that list...

In <3m3qi8$t6b@crl.crl.com>, lfiction@crl.com (Jonathan Zamick)  wrote:

> Actually I'd be very interested in this as well. Go certainly can be
> used as a bit of a substitute, but DenDen...

Yes, denden must be very different, probably closer to checkers or
backgammon (i.e. a game of movement, probably simpler than chess).
I read an encyclopedia of world games, and I can't quite remember,
but the largest class of games is called something like Albuquerque.
They are race and capture games with a limited set of counters, usually
stones or seeds, placed and moved on positions connected by lines.
I think 9-man's morris is an example.  Hardly any modern games are of this
class, but they are still popular in the southern hemisphere and
Central Asia.  I would not at all be surpised if denden was in that
family.

>Both are true: they are "irregular" in tye sense that they do not use the
>complex, highly disciplined formations employed bby the Five Empires, but
>they also have uniforms and weapons supplied by the cities and towns that
>supply them. So their uniforms are "regular." They just lack training but
>make up for it in bravery and ferocity. Don't limit troops to either
>illustrations or to the brief descriptions: the Ghatoni fight well with
>swords, but they also have light spears. This is one of the signs of their
>"irregularity."
>
>-- 
>M. A. R. Barker

Thanks very much. That helps clear up some of our confusion, much of which is
based upon assumptions about Tekumel which are drawn from Earth history. 
Tekumel is, of course, very different, but where we lack information we fill in
as  best we can.

With regard to the swords and javelins: we have two options. We can (1) class
them as Swordsmen equipped with javelins (javelins in our rules do not confer
a ranged missile capability, but add to initial impact and the enhanced effects
of supporting rear ranks), or (2) we can call them Spearmen. I actually favor
option (1), which lets them hit harder on the initial round.

Part of the reason for going into this detail is that a friend is really
interested in building a Ghatoni army in 15mm. He is even willing to commission
Richard Kerr, onetime head of Ral Partha's sculpting section, to sculpt
Ghatoni infantry and Serudla for him.

Thanks again.

Rob

From MC4435@mclink.it Fri Apr 14 18:59:06 CDT 1995

Subject: Religions And Frp
Date: Wed, 12 Apr 95 20:18:08 CEST
Organization: MC-link - Italian Dial-Up Online Service
Lines: 40
Message-ID: <797710688.MC4435@mclink.it>
NNTP-Posting-Host: ax433.mclink.it


rdushay@delphi.com wrote:

 > Debates over imaginary religions can get so fascinating.  Piermaria
 > may have made a mistake in assuming that a religion has to make sense

I've not explained myself too well... :-)

We started with a "scientific" explanation of the world (genesis and
phisical laws) and we inferred as this world might have been influenced
the philosophies and religions of the population of this world.
It is somewhat of a limited point of view, I admit, but we can't be
omniscent and consider all factors determining and influencing the
religious thoughts of a population!

And as to make sense: we developed the thought that RPs are not (no
more) spawn of simulation games, they have developed in a new narrative
form (closer to movie, poem, literature, thatre... than to war games or
board games) and in this view, I think all worlds you may imagine of
with this narrative form, should make sense "narratively". If your genre
is "nonsense", "nonsense" should make sense... If your genre is "classic
heroic fantasy", you should be bind to what you would be expected to
find in "classic heroic fantasy" (think of Conan or tLotR): black and
white, all or nothing, hero or honourless villain.

 > of awe and mystery to it, a feeling that some things must be left to

Religion may be also self exploration, fear, superstition,
illumination... you may explore one thousand of aspects of religion in
one thousand of campaigns...

 >   Just my 1.5 cents.                          --Bob Dushay

Just my 1 cent, or less! :-)

 >                                     Dritlan, Legion of Obscure Books
 >                                     1st Imperial Heavy Scholars

Ave atque Vale, amice!
                        ait anuas  [Ex Arcano]

Summary: Check out _The Eye of All-Seeing Wonder_
Keywords: tekumel, ept

Bill Cumberland says Tekumel needs a magazine.

Anyone one this list who doesn't know of it should check out
_The Eye of All-Seeing Wonder_.  It is consistently excellent.

Issue #4 came out about a month ago.  It is on a semi-regular
schedule; expect 3-4 issues a year.

The Eye is produced, and mainly written, in the UK.  David
Morris was the editor for the first four issues, but he has 
just announced Steve Foster will be taking over.

Articles have included:
Detailed clan write-ups (2-3 pgs), Adventure scenarios, 
rules opions (GURPS-Tekumel is popular), Cultural pieces,
and a slow chapter-by-chapter release of _Tirikelu_, 
Morris' Pendragon/GURPS-ish rules custom-tailored for
Tekumel.

Subscriptions:
3L per issue (UK)
3.50L per issue (Europe)
$6 per issue (USA)

Send money to:
Steve Foster
26 Western Lane
Balham
London
SW12 8JS
United Kingdom

Issues #3 & 4 still available from Steve.
Copies of issues #1 & 2 available from 

Andrew Lorince   (famed US Tekumelyani fan & games dealer)
862 Flemington Ave
Pittsburgh, PA  15217
USA

I practically guarantee you will like this fanzine if
you're a real Tekumel fan.

Best,
-DC

Organization: University of Minnesota, Twin Cities
X-Newsreader: Value-Added NewsWatcher 2.0b24.0+
References: <3lvtmt$h0r@newsbf02.news.aol.com>
Date: Wed, 12 Apr 1995 17:10:58 GMT
Lines: 23

In article <3lvtmt$h0r@newsbf02.news.aol.com>, mabelrode@aol.com
(Mabelrode) wrote:

>       I am wondering if I could get the statistics for Hokun riding beast
> and a list of some other prefered weapons of the Hokun.

The Hokun riding beasts are about as intelligent as Earth warhorses -- not
much more. They can bite and strike with their modified feet (they are
quadripedal, with the top pair of arms atrophied and used for feeding),
but they don't use weapons or have the intelligence to speak or act much
on their own. They are hunched, swaybacked so that a kind of saddle can be
put on them, and still cloudily translucent, though not as transparent as
their masters are. The Hokun are rather set in their preferences for
weapons: long "glass" lances, swords, and armour that is made of the same
extruded stuff their weapons are made of. They have daggers, crossbows,
some magic, and are also ale to use the weapons of the Ancients found in
ruined cities here and there. Their riding beasts are quite fast and are
able to run down a human being. I can't give you better statistics than
this, unfortunately. The Hokun are dangerous opponents, and my gaming
groups give them a wide berth!

-- 
M. A. R. Barker

From resmith@donald.cc.utexas.edu Fri Apr 14 18:59:14 CDT 1995
Article: 296 of alt.games.frp.tekumel
Path: news.io.com!news.tamu.edu!cs.utexas.edu!geraldo.cc.utexas.edu!donald.cc.utexas.edu!not-for-mail
From: resmith@donald.cc.utexas.edu (Robert Smith)
Newsgroups: alt.games.frp.tekumel
Subject: Fungi on Tekumel
Date: 13 Apr 1995 12:02:31 -0500
Organization: The University of Texas at Austin; Austin, Texas
Lines: 55
Message-ID: <3mjlf7$gsj@donald.cc.utexas.edu>
NNTP-Posting-Host: donald.cc.utexas.edu

I was flipping through a travel magazine the other day and came upon an article
on fungi. This got me to thinking about the use of fungi as food on Tekumel.
I began to wonder if this might have some religious connotations. Some of the
ideas I have begun to develop are as follows:
 
Fungi and Religion
Certain religions, in particular the temples of Sarku and Durritlamish, view
even be cultivated, harvested, and sold fresh or dried and exported.
fungi as sacred food. They spring from dead things, so must be a gift of the
God of death and undeath. These fungi might be consumed in rituals, some of
which might contribute to religious visions. They could also be cultivated
in the underworlds and consumed as delicacies or common food.
 
I feel that the temples of Hru'u and Ksarul would also favor the consumption
of fungi, and might even purchase hallucinogenic varieties from the Sarku and
Durrit temples. They would not view it as such a holy food, however. The
temples of Belkhanu and Qon might also favor these foods.
 
I see the temples of Vimuhla and Chiteng as completely eschewing fungi, as the
spawn of Sarku and his servant Missum. I am not sure what doctrinal opinion
might be held by worshippers of Avanthe and Dilinalia, although I am of the
opinion that Avanthe temples would take no particular position. Most other
temples would take no interest in this question.
 
Where to Find Fungi
Just like on earth, fungus grow better in damp, cool conditions. Thus, the
underworlds would be outstanding locations for growth of fungi. Certain
locales would also breed fungi well, such as around Purdimal, where the
variety of different types would be staggering. In some of these are type may
even be cultivated, harvested, and sold fresh or dried and exported.
 
Uses of Fungi
Fungal growths may be used for food, to make drugs, and in religious services
as mentioned above. Some types, found only in the deepest underworlds or in
the furthest reaches of the barbarian mountains, may sell as delicacies for
hundreds of kaitars for a single gram. Especially those that not only taste
good, but impart "altered reality" to the eater. Other varieties may be ground
into powders and used as spices or consumed as drugs for medicinal or
recreational purposes.
 
Appearances of Fungi
Tekumelyani fungi are at least as diverse as those found on earth. An edible
mushroom particularly favored by Sarku worshippers and found only in the
underworld is known as the Deathhead, because it vaguely resembles as
small human skull. Another, the Scepter of Belkhanu, appears as a long staff
topped by a small golden knob. Some edible fungi have disguised themselves as
food of the Ssu, so the collector must be very careful in his attempts to
harvest.
 
Just some initial thoughts on an admittedly odd topic.
Any additional thoughts or comments are welcome and encouraged. Maybe I'll
experiment with some recipes and post those as well.
 
Rob

NNTP-Posting-Host: ix-dfw1-19.ix.netcom.com

In <3mk4j7$sm4@cedar.mr.net> alberti@riverside.mr.net (Bob Alberti)
writes: 
>
[snip]
>Likewise for Gods.  Gods are by definition insane, because sanity is
>relative.  Are WE sane, to ants?  Can they discern our purposes? 
[snip]

you've fallen and you can't get up.

#1.  Reality is not bound to observe our definitions.  It is not
relative.  It is not based on our perceptions.

#2.  Sanity and Insanity are legal definitions.  An insane person is
one judged to be legally incapable of taking responsibility for their
actions.  Lots of crazy people are not (yet?) certified insane.

#3.  While I believe there is One God, others may differ.  However,
one's ability or inability to discern purpose does not affect the
sanity or insanity of God.  It might affect the perceiver's sanity.

#4.  Religions and FRP are tightly coupled.  What kind of FRP does not
have a church, temple, monastery, priest, acolyte, healer, shaman, or
philosopher?  I submit it would be boring.

#5.  Professor Barker has excellently described the religions of
Tekumel in his gaming material, DAW paperbacks, and magazine articles,
columns, and interviews.  

Use your imagination.  DEFINE A PURPOSE IF YOU CANNOT EASILY FIND ONE. 
Then use scientific methods to fit your model to your fantasy world's
reality.  Let your players discover how it works by playing the game.

Perhaps your initial model was based on a get-rich-quick-scheme.  Then
you discover (hopefully through your players) that there is a God or
Gods (or not?) and they want to get even with the charlatans (or the
con artists want the party for exposing their charade).  Or whatever.

Troll (Troll@nochet.demon.co.uk) wrote:
: GURPS Mad World also showed a world where gods were very real, but insane,
: so that people actively avoided them.

I once commented that the President of the United States was insane.
The person I was talking to looked at me and insisted to know what I 
meant by that.  I replied, "Because he thinks he's qualified to be
President of the United States."

Someone who thinks he's smart enough to run the world must be insane.

Likewise for Gods.  Gods are by definition insane, because sanity is
relative.  Are WE sane, to ants?  Can they discern our purposes? 
Can they avoid our feet or our shovels?  Do they understand why one
moment life is checked cloth and soda pop, and the next thing they
know they're being crushed en masse?

Gods are insane, and they can't be avoided.

In article <3mkkmf$vra@usenetw1.news.prodigy.com>, AEVF56A@prodigy.com
(Shelby Michlin) wrote:

> And I'm shocked. I have a few volumes of the older rules versions, but

> Another expensive, misleading purchase. I'm very disappointed. I pull out 
> the Player's Guide, and every page refers me to previously-published, not-
> included, SOLO ADVENTURES? For RULES?  What are you thinking??? 
> I'm extremely disappointed. I liked what I've read of Tekumel, I read the 
> Daw books, and when I learned about this new boxed set of rules I thought 
> I'd finally have something COMPLETE to enjoy. Frankly, this seems more 
> like a scam than anything else. In fifteen years of role-playing I've 
> never been taken like this. I don't care how intriguing the world is; 
> this was a BIG mistake.

>   SHELBY  AEVF56A@prodigy.com

Sorry you didn't like the set. I have been pleading with TOME to reissue
the Sourcebook in its entirety (3 vols.). They say they can only afford it
if the new rules sell. They could not afford a bigger and more
comprehensive rules set either since it would put the price of the set way
above what ANYBODY would be willing to pay for it! Tekumel is just too
complex a world to be jammed into one "world book." or rules set. The solo
adventures are referred to because they contain BACKGROUND. The new
published rules have all one needs for combat, movement, magic, magical
items, nonplayer characters, and the like. I wish there were some way to
give folks like you a sourcebook and a rules set all in the same bundle,
but, as I said, the price in today's publishing world would be WAY out of
sight. I tried to be more "complete" in the first "Empire of the Petal
Throne" rules, but they were riddled with gaps and "easy" compromises.
There's no intention to "scam" you. Believe me, we worked hard to provide
a set of RULES that covers enough to adventure with. If you need
BACKGROUND, you can refer to the solo adventures: they do fit into the
"rules" since they produce rounded player characters with personal
histories, talents, possessions, etc. who fit into the world. What TOME
has just issued is meant to be RULES for those who need to codify and
quantify their parties' actions and set up nonplayer characters and
scenarios with which to interact. It is manifestly impossible to fit ALL
of Tekumel into such a single boxed set!

A game is a simulation of an environment plus some sort of story line that
leads toward a goal. Monopoly, for example is a very simplistic picture of
Atlantic City, New Jersey, in the early thirties. It can be put into one
box in its entirety because the simulation leaves out vast amounts of
"irrelevant" data that could have enhanced a more complex game: property
taxes, city government structures, federal and state institutions,
corruption at various levels, all sorts of "politics," etc. When
roleplaying games got started, Arneson and Gygax put all that they wanted
to simulate into three booklets in one little box. People -- myself
included -- kept seeing more and more things we wanted to add to their
simulation. I took my world of Tekumel out of mothballs and decided to
make a "game" out of it. (I had "gamed" Tekumel in the forties but without
codified, written rules.) My first published game, "Empire of the Petal
Throne," contained more features than most other contemporary roleplaying
attempts of the time: Tekumel's clans, religions, governments, social
structures, etc. were all given all-too-brief descriptions and were
slapped into the rules-set. Many features had to be left out. As time
passed, and players kept demanding more details, the world became much
more complicated -- and difficult to describe in any short fashion. Now
it's so intricate that I have to keep character lists in my computer
database, and another author is currently preparing a tome (no pun
intended) on the Tekumalani deities and their sects.

I am sorry we had to do it this way. Many who already have backgrounds in
Tekumel will find these rules easy to use. They will already own a lot of
the previously published materials. Beginners will not require much more
than what is presented here to develop introductory adventures. You seem
to be in an intermediate group who want (and deserve) more source material
and were expecting a single boxed game to contain the whole world of
Tekumel, PLUS rules. I wish I could help. You can ask TOME for your money
back -- or you can bug them to republish the Sourcebook so you'll have all
the details of the various societies of the planet.  When you consider
that certain other major roleplaying games cost five hundred dollars or
more to acquire even a large portion of the sourcebooks, playing aids,
scenario packs, etc. etc., I don't see this as such a bad deal.

What I am coming to is this: the new game is a set of RULES (plus some
background materials, spells, magic items, etc.). It is NOT a "complete"
game in the sense that Monopoly is a "complete" simulation of the
extracted features of an imaginary "Atlantic City." Very few roleplaying
games these days are "complete" in that holistic sense. Most require
"world books" or game aids or other forms of background material, and the
complex ones require further modules to present distant regions, minor
societies, special species, intricate political and social details that
would not be enjoyed by all (e.g. the mechanics of buying and running a
fief, politicking in the higher circles of the ruler's court, etc.). These
are in addition to the usual "dungeon" modules that fill up the
game-stores.

Well, I wish you were happier. But one cannot please everyody. By all
means, please return your game to TOME and ask for your money back. If
they don't agree, let me know and I'll see what I can do.

-- 
M. A. R. Barker

I just recieved teh new rules and was suprised that no generation rules for
standard humans were present.  I own these rules separately from the "solo"
adventures, but they should have been included.  So far what little I have
browsed seems to be nicely done.  The artwork is pleasant, and volume 3 is of
great use in running a game.  The first two volumes reprint much of S&G volume
2, while these are somewhat clearer I was hoping for more.  Also no non/semi
intelligent beasties included in rules, although I again have the Bestiary. 
This is not as important as character rules which really should have been
included.  I know TOME is a business, and they already have these rules out,
but a stripped down version could have easily been included.  The map is very
nice, as are all the maps ever printed for Tekumel games.  I do like the
archetypes and their illustrations.  A little bit
of background and an index would also have been nice. As it stands it is quite
nice for those of us who already own previous materials, but lack too much for
those interested in it as a new game.


Seth Friedman

From: wdf@netcom.com (William Faulkner)
Subject: REVIEW - Gardasiyal The new RPG rules (long)
Message-ID: <wdf-1504950002010001@192.0.2.1>
Sender: wdf@netcom20.netcom.com
Organization: NETCOM On-line Communication Services (408 261-4700 guest)
References: <3mkkmf$vra@usenetw1.news.prodigy.com> <barke002-1404951247190001@dialup-4-2.gw.umn.edu>
Date: Sat, 15 Apr 1995 07:02:01 GMT
Lines: 178

Well, just got home and found the new Tekumel RPG rules waiting on my
doorstep. Boy was I excited, it felt like Christmas. So as a service to
others, I am sharing my initial impressions of the new rules.

I must admit that I am disapointed with the results of Gardasiyal. I
acknowledge all the work that people put in writing it, but I feel that
given the current state of the art in role-playing rules, a much better
product could have been produced (not that I want to try it though). But
first, for those who care, I will review the new rules.

What's included:
   Three stapled-bound, glossy magazine-covered rule books, Vol 1 is the        
   player's guide. Vol 2 is Sorcery & Spells, Vol 3 is the Referee's Guide.
   A two sided, full color, glossy map of the five empires. Covers the same 
   area as the one in S&G, but no hexes, but much better graphics.
   A few staple bound sheets that make up the Character Archetypes and the 
   Player's Character Sheets.
   Two 10-sided dice, and two 6-sided dice.
   A full color box to keep everything in.

What's not included:
   No background of Tekumel
   No descriptions of the creatures met in Tekumel (you need the Tekumel 
      Besitary for this, although their stats are included).
   No rules for creating basic characters (You need Vol 1 of Adv in Tekumel)

I personally feel that all three should be included in some form,
especially character creation. It is INCOMPLETE with out this. Imagine a
new player to Tekumel, and you buy the product and find that you can't
even create a character! You have to buy a seperate book that you can't
even find anymore. Come on TOME, loosen up and include Vol 1 of Adventures
in Tekumel in the box. It is required for playing the game!

A brief description of the world (ala EPT) could have been easily
included. 5-6 pages ain't going to break the bank. And the creatures could
have been somewhat descibed, at least the major intellegent non-humans.
Give me a break. OK enough bitching about what's not in the game. What
about that is in the box.

First an overall comment on the 3 books. Compared to most other RPG's the
rule books are fairly cheaply constructed. Simple staple binding and a
glossy cover. About the same as Deluxe RQ books that AH brought out 10
years ago. The internal layout is not very good either. No index, no table
of contents, and a very poor look. The artwork as a whole reminds me of
RPG's of the late 70's some of it good, most fairly poor. 

Volume 1, the Player's guide, is 46 pages long and has seven sections.
Section 1 contains rules on modifing characters rolled up with the Vol 1
of Adventures in Tekumel so that non-humans can be played. (Yea
non-humans, Boo! I need another book for basic character generation and
skill lists, actually a very LOUD BOO!)

Section 2 contains extensive price list of goods, including arms, armor,
adventuring gear, clothing, jewellery, gems, food & beverages, drugs,
poisons, lodging, housing costs (including building your own clan-house),
transport & shipping prices, slaves, and entertainers. Very nice and
detailed. Great work.

Section 3 contain the movement rules, tactical and strategic. Seems OK.

Section 4 is combat. Two systems are presented a quick play version and a
more detailed hit-point version. It's basically a re-done, simplified
version of the S&G rules. The initiative modifiers are nice, e.g attacking
with a long spear gives one a +5 to initiative (on a D20) for the first
round, but a -5 on the remaining rounds. Overall my impression of the
combat rules is, while better than S&G's cumbersome system, are still too
chart dependant. You compare you HBS vs your opponent's to get your basic
chance to hit, modify that with a rather long list of modifiers and roll
to hit. If you hit, you then find what combat table you use, and then roll
on it. In the Quick Play (QP) your hits can have either no effect, minor,
serious, critical, or kill results. If playing with Hit Points, you take
the wound result and roll again to determine hit points lost. Whew! A nice
section on NPC morale is included, as well as a healing section and a
section on mass combat (not for military battles, but rather fights with
more than about 10 combatants per side.

Section 5 contains guidelines for other adventure actions, use of skills,
etc. A very light coverage of the subject.

Section 6 covers experience points and how to use them. I have a personal
dislike for most EP system, and this one is no exception, especially since
it rewards characters for defeating oppenents only. I thought this type of
system went out with basic D&D. It is extremely disappointing that the
"officia" Tekumel rules gives experience only for killing things. I
thought RPG's had developed beyond that. Throw that system out and just
give PC's EP's for accomplishing tasks, not for killing every little
Dri-ant they step on.

Section 7 is Income and Expenditures. It is simple, but seems quite
adequate. I like simple.

Volume 2 is the spell book. It is 62 pages long, with two sections. 
Section 8 contains the rules of spell casting a simplified version of the
S&G rules.

Section 9 is the spell corpus It appears that all the spells from S&G are
here.   I personally consider the spells the crowing achievement of the
S&G rules, and they are repeated here and once again, outshine everything
else.

Volume 3 is the Referee's guide, the longest book at 78 pages with 6 sections.
Section 10 is Encounters. Random encounter charts for any occasion.
Encounter charts are a crutch for the GM, but can be useful for getting an
idea of the creatures roaming around in certain areas. Rules for surprise
and amush are included here. 

Section 11 Covers basic rules for NPC's, including NPC reactions and
personality traits and random tables for special equipment they may have.

Section 12 is the Treasure section. It includes charts for what type of
treasure is found in certain areas, e.g. Animal lairs, Mercants & Nobles,
etc.  A rather worthless section. Once again, a throw-back to the orginal
D&D rules.

Section 13 details the magic items: 45 eyes, 31 amulets, 30 misc magic
items and 31 magic books are covered in great detail. Rules for magic
scrolls, magic arms and armor are also covered. Very nice and seems to be
quite complete. At 38 pages it is the longest section in the book and 2nd
longest in the entire set. M'mm spells take 50 pages and magic items 38. I
guess we know where Prof Barker's interests lie (and I absolutely agree).

Section 14 is the standard EPT divine intervention rules. Simple, hard to
get, and watch out for retribution.

Section 15 has some basic ideas for adventures to run PC's through. A nice
section to have, and done fairly well.

The map as mentioned above is two-sided, full-color, glossy 17"x21" in
size. It is very nice. No hexes, so keep the old map around for distances,
but I love this map. Some real nice cartography really adds to the feel of
the world. You can really appreciate the difficulty the Tsolyani faced in
invading Yan Kor. The front contains Livyanu, Shenyu, Mu'ugalavya, Yan
Kor, Tsolyanu, and the western part of Salarvya. The reverse contains the
remainder of Salarvya, THe plains of Glass, Mihallu, and the rest. No new
territory, just the same as the old map but completely different. The map
is also surrounded by glyphs of the 10 gods and 10 cohorts on front and
other stylized symbols on the back. I'm not sure exactly what they are but
they are pretty.

The box is full color, both top and bottom. The front shows some
adventures encountering the Hokun via tube-car and they aren't in good
shape. Unfortunately, I don't like this particular piece of artwork. The
back is much nicer (TOME used it in their flyer) and is a picture of a
young girl being dressed with a city with its pyarmids in the background.
Very nice.

Over all I rate this RPG as very disappointing. I don't feel ripped-off
and deceived like a previous poster, but after waiting so long I am quite
sad, since this game won't sell. People expect more from their game
systems these days. It would have been a great achievement for 1975, but
for 1995, it is third-rate. I can forgive the somewhat cheap components.
Tekumel has limited appeal and TOME is hedging their investment. Gamers
expect more than is given in this set. Non-Tekumel fans won't touch this
game, and why should they, when vastly superior products are on the market
at a lower cost. I was very excited when I got home this evening and found
that the game had arrived. Now I am very sad, since I see this as the end
of the line for Tekumel products. This game won't sell at all well, thus
TOME will give up on Tekumel, sigh! What I thought was a new beginning,
now seems like the end. Oh, well, I will go out and buy another one, It
will be a real collector's item some day. Not many people will buy it. 
I can't blame them, but I am very disappointed.

My severe critisism of the product should not be taken in any form as an
attack on the authors, contributors, or TOME. I appreciate their effort,
and I know how much work was put into it. I was hoping for a product that
could re-vitalize the gaming world of Tekumel to outsiders. Unfortunately,
the game is only suitable to those already sold on the world. Heavy Sigh!
The map, the magic spells, and magic items are great. But it isn't enough.
More had to be done. There needed to be more content. It needed to be a
complete game that a newbie could get hooked on. As it stands now, a
neophyte who bought this will feel ripped-off and that is too bad. I'm at
a loss for words. It feels that I am now writting an ephitate for Tekumel.
I hope this in not the case. I love the world too much.

Well, I've been a bit long winded, but its time to end this post.  It will
be interesting to see how others feel about the new game.

Bill Faulkner

setnaffa (setnaffa@ix.netcom.com) wrote:
: In <3mk4j7$sm4@cedar.mr.net> alberti@riverside.mr.net (Bob Alberti)
: writes: 
: >Likewise for Gods.  Gods are by definition insane, because sanity is
: >relative.  Are WE sane, to ants?  Can they discern our purposes? 

: you've fallen and you can't get up.

: #1.  Reality is not bound to observe our definitions.  It is not
: relative.  It is not based on our perceptions.

So YOU say, oh product of rigid Western thought.  Other philosophies
might disagree with you.  *I* think reality is in fact flexible...

: #2.  Sanity and Insanity are legal definitions.  An insane person is
: one judged to be legally incapable of taking responsibility for their
: actions.  Lots of crazy people are not (yet?) certified insane.

Legal definitions are subsets of meaning.  Before there were laws,
was no one insane?  I am not speaking of the "legal" definition
of sanity, but of its practical definition.

: #3.  While I believe there is One God, others may differ.  However,
: one's ability or inability to discern purpose does not affect the
: sanity or insanity of God.  It might affect the perceiver's sanity.

Gods are insane precisely because their goals and motives are not
bound to human strictures.  A god might say with perfect certainty,
"The reason that suffering exists is because those yellow flowers are
discordant and Tuesday."  For all practical human purposes, that god
is insane, despite the fact that as a god, they are undoubtedy correct.

Part of the reason that I am an atheist is my belief that a "real" god
would be entirely moot to human existence.  They could no more meaninfully
interact with me than I could with a blood cell.  If a god cannot interact
with the universe, than Occam's razor suggests that gods aren't there at all.

: #4.  Religions and FRP are tightly coupled.  What kind of FRP does not
: have a church, temple, monastery, priest, acolyte, healer, shaman, or
: philosopher?  I submit it would be boring.

The FRP's that HAVE churches, temples, monasteries, etc ARE BORING, by-
and-large.  They treat religion like a McDonald's of the soul.  Here's
your revivified comrade, would you like fries with that?  Thank you for
the sacrifice of $100 gold pieces.  In return, here is a miraculous 
cure for your lost limb.

: #5.  Professor Barker has excellently described the religions of
: Tekumel in his gaming material, DAW paperbacks, and magazine articles,
: columns, and interviews.  

Yes.

From barke002@maroon.tc.umn.edu Sun Apr 16 20:13:32 CDT 1995
Article: 315 of alt.games.frp.tekumel
Newsgroups: alt.games.frp.tekumel

From: barke002@maroon.tc.umn.edu
Subject: Re: New Rules, No chaaracter generation

Sender: news@news.cis.umn.edu (Usenet News Administration)

Organization: University of Minnesota, Twin Cities
